    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Chromosome-scale genome assembly of sweet cherry ( Prunus avium L.) cv. Tieton obtained using long-read and Hi-C sequencing"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-08-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Sweet cherry (Prunus avium) is an economically significant fruit species in the genus Prunus. However, in contrast to other important fruit trees in this genus, only one draft genome assembly is available for sweet cherry, which was assembled using only Illumina short-read sequences. The incompleteness and low quality of the current sweet cherry draft genome limit its use in genetic and genomic studies. A high-quality chromosome-scale sweet cherry reference genome assembly is therefore needed. A total of 65.05&#8201;Gb of Oxford Nanopore long reads and 46.24&#8201;Gb of Illumina short reads were generated, representing ~190x and 136x coverage, respectively, of the sweet cherry genome. The final de novo assembly resulted in a phased haplotype assembly of 344.29&#8201;Mb with a contig N50 of 3.25&#8201;Mb. Hi-C scaffolding of the genome resulted in eight pseudochromosomes containing 99.59% of the bases in the assembled genome. Genome annotation revealed that more than half of the genome (59.40%) was composed of repetitive sequences, and 40,338 protein-coding genes were predicted, 75.40% of which were functionally annotated. With the chromosome-scale assembly, we revealed that gene duplication events contributed to the expansion of gene families for salicylic acid/jasmonic acid carboxyl methyltransferase and ankyrin repeat-containing proteins in the genome of sweet cherry. Four auxin-responsive genes (two GH3s and two SAURs) were induced in the late stage of fruit development, indicating that auxin is crucial for the sweet cherry ripening process. In addition, 772 resistance genes were identified and functionally predicted in the sweet cherry genome. The high-quality genome assembly of sweet cherry obtained in this study will provide valuable genomic resources for sweet cherry improvement and molecular breeding."/>

    <meta name="description" content="Sweet cherry (Prunus avium) is an economically significant fruit species in the genus Prunus. However, in contrast to other important fruit trees in this genus, only one draft genome assembly is available for sweet cherry, which was assembled using only Illumina short-read sequences. The incompleteness and low quality of the current sweet cherry draft genome limit its use in genetic and genomic studies. A high-quality chromosome-scale sweet cherry reference genome assembly is therefore needed. A total of 65.05&#8201;Gb of Oxford Nanopore long reads and 46.24&#8201;Gb of Illumina short reads were generated, representing ~190x and 136x coverage, respectively, of the sweet cherry genome. The final de novo assembly resulted in a phased haplotype assembly of 344.29&#8201;Mb with a contig N50 of 3.25&#8201;Mb. Hi-C scaffolding of the genome resulted in eight pseudochromosomes containing 99.59% of the bases in the assembled genome. Genome annotation revealed that more than half of the genome (59.40%) was composed of repetitive sequences, and 40,338 protein-coding genes were predicted, 75.40% of which were functionally annotated. With the chromosome-scale assembly, we revealed that gene duplication events contributed to the expansion of gene families for salicylic acid/jasmonic acid carboxyl methyltransferase and ankyrin repeat-containing proteins in the genome of sweet cherry. Four auxin-responsive genes (two GH3s and two SAURs) were induced in the late stage of fruit development, indicating that auxin is crucial for the sweet cherry ripening process. In addition, 772 resistance genes were identified and functionally predicted in the sweet cherry genome. The high-quality genome assembly of sweet cherry obtained in this study will provide valuable genomic resources for sweet cherry improvement and molecular breeding."/>

            <li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/genomics" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Genomics</a></li><li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/plant-genetics" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Plant genetics</a></li>
        </ul>
    </div>

                
    

    

            </div>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Sweet cherry (<i>Prunus avium</i>) is an economically significant fruit species in the genus <i>Prunus</i>. However, in contrast to other important fruit trees in this genus, only one draft genome assembly is available for sweet cherry, which was assembled using only Illumina short-read sequences. The incompleteness and low quality of the current sweet cherry draft genome limit its use in genetic and genomic studies. A high-quality chromosome-scale sweet cherry reference genome assembly is therefore needed. A total of 65.05âGb of Oxford Nanopore long reads and 46.24âGb of Illumina short reads were generated, representing ~190x and 136x coverage, respectively, of the sweet cherry genome. The final de novo assembly resulted in a phased haplotype assembly of 344.29âMb with a contig N50 of 3.25âMb. Hi-C scaffolding of the genome resulted in eight pseudochromosomes containing 99.59% of the bases in the assembled genome. Genome annotation revealed that more than half of the genome (59.40%) was composed of repetitive sequences, and 40,338 protein-coding genes were predicted, 75.40% of which were functionally annotated. With the chromosome-scale assembly, we revealed that gene duplication events contributed to the expansion of gene families for salicylic acid/jasmonic acid carboxyl methyltransferase and ankyrin repeat-containing proteins in the genome of sweet cherry. Four auxin-responsive genes (two GH3s and two SAURs) were induced in the late stage of fruit development, indicating that auxin is crucial for the sweet cherry ripening process. In addition, 772 resistance genes were identified and functionally predicted in the sweet cherry genome. The high-quality genome assembly of sweet cherry obtained in this study will provide valuable genomic resources for sweet cherry improvement and molecular breeding.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Sweet cherry (<i>Prunus avium</i>), originating from the Caspian and Black Sea regions, is an economically significant fruit species worldwide. Annual global sweet cherry production is ~2.2 million tons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Quero-GarcÃ­a, J., Iezzoni, A., PuÅawska, J. &amp; Lang, G. Cherries: Botany, Production and Uses 1â533 (CABI PUBLISHING, Boston, MA, 2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d37401e575">1</a></sup>. Sweet cherry was first introduced to China in the 1870s, and its widespread cultivation began in the 1990s. In the last year, the total planting area of sweet cherry in China reached 233,300âha, with production of ~1.2 million tons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Xuwei, D. et al. Fruit scientific research in New China in the past 70 years: cherry. J. Fruit. Sci.36, 1339â1351 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d37401e579">2</a></sup>. In addition, there have been dedicated breeding efforts for this crop. The traditional breeding cycle of a sweet cherry cultivar is more than 15 years long, and marker-assisted selection and genomic selection are current candidate strategies for speeding up the breeding cycle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Garcia, J. Q. Cherry breeding in the world: current analysis and future perspectives. Italus Hortus26, 9â20 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d37401e583">3</a></sup>. Although sweet cherry has a simple, compact genome (2<i>n</i>â=â2<i>x</i>â=â16), only one draft genome assembly has been reported to date<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding. DNA Res.24, 499â508 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d37401e594">4</a></sup>. Owing to the use of short-read sequencing technology, the draft genome assembly exhibited a small scaffold N50 of 219.6âKb and a low genome coverage of 77.8%.</p><p>Within the <i>Prunus</i> genus, the Chinese plum (Mei, <i>Prunus mume</i>) genome was first sequenced in 2012<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zhang, Q. et al. The genome of Prunus mume. Nat. Commun.3, 1318 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d37401e607">5</a></sup>. Since then, several other important fruit species have been sequenced, such as peach (<i>Prunus persica</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Verde, I. et al. The Peach v2.0 release: high-resolution linkage mapping and deep resequencing improve chromosome-scale assembly and contiguity. BMC Genomics.18, 225 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d37401e614">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="International Peach Genome, I. et al. The high-quality draft genome of peach (Prunus persica) identifies unique patterns of genetic diversity, domestication and genome evolution. Nat. Genet.45, 487â494 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d37401e617">7</a></sup>, sweet cherry (<i>Prunus avium</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding. DNA Res.24, 499â508 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d37401e625">4</a></sup>, almond (<i>Prunus dulcis</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Sanchez-Perez, R. et al. Mutation of a bHLH transcription factor allowed almond domestication. Science364, 1095â1098 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d37401e632">8</a></sup>, flowering cherry (<i>Prunus yedoensis</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Baek, S. et al. Draft genome sequence of wild Prunus yedoensis reveals massive inter-specific hybridization between sympatric flowering cherries. Genome Biol.19, 127 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37401e639">9</a></sup>, a flowering cherry interspecific hybrid (âSomei-Yoshinoâ, <i>Cerasus</i>âÃâ<i>yedoensis</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. Phased genome sequence of an interspecific hybrid flowering cherry, âSomei-Yoshinoâ (Cerasus Ã yedoensis). DNA Res.26, 379â389 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d37401e650">10</a></sup>, apricot (<i>Prunus armeniaca</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jiang, F. et al. The apricot (Prunus armeniaca L.) genome elucidates Rosaceae evolution and beta-carotenoid synthesis. Horticulture Res.6, 128 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37401e657">11</a></sup> and European plum (<i>Prunus domestica</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Zhebentyayeva, T. et al. Genetic characterization of worldwide Prunus domestica (plum) germplasm using sequence-based genotyping. Hortic. Res.6, 12 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d37401e665">12</a></sup>. All the sequenced genomes exhibit high coverage, ranging from 84.6% in Chinese plum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zhang, Q. et al. The genome of Prunus mume. Nat. Commun.3, 1318 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d37401e669">5</a></sup> to 126% in flowering cherry (<i>P. yedoensis</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Baek, S. et al. Draft genome sequence of wild Prunus yedoensis reveals massive inter-specific hybridization between sympatric flowering cherries. Genome Biol.19, 127 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37401e676">9</a></sup>, with the exception of the genome assembly of sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki (77.8%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding. DNA Res.24, 499â508 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d37401e680">4</a></sup>. In other assemblies, including those of peach, almond, apricot, and Chinese plum, chromosome-level scaffolds with fewer gaps than in the assembly of sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki have also been constructed.</p><p>In this study, we aimed to assemble a high-quality chromosome-scale reference genome for sweet cherry using Oxford Nanopore technology (ONT) combined with short Illumina sequencing reads and Hi-C scaffolding. The high-quality genome assembly of sweet cherry will facilitate molecular breeding of sweet cherry and advance our understanding of the genetics and evolution of the <i>Prunus</i> genus.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and assembly of the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome</h3><p>The sweet cherry cultivar âTietonâ (cv. Tieton) was used for whole-genome sequencing and chromosome-scale assembly. After filtering low-quality reads, a total of 65.05âGb of Oxford Nanopore long reads and 46.24âGb of Illumina short reads were obtained. The sequencing details are provided in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S1</a>.</p><p>Several approaches were applied to assemble the genome of sweet cherry cv. Tieton. The assembly statistics and Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCOs, v3.0.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Seppey, M., Manni, M. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness. Methods Mol. Biol.1962, 227â245 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d37401e708">13</a></sup> analysis results are shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#Tab1">1</a>. The NECAT-medaka-NextPolish-Purge_Haplotigs assembly was chosen for further analysis because it showed the best quality. The genome assembly of sweet cherry cv. Tieton exhibited a total size of 344.29âMb, consisting of 610 contigs with a contig N50 size of 3.25âMb and a largest contig size of 13.6âMb. The statistics for our sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome assembly are shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#Tab2">2</a>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of different assemblies and BUSCO analysis results for the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Statistics of the genome assembly for sweet cherry (<i>Prunus avium</i>) cv. Tieton</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Hi-C scaffolding generated a total of 134,298,049 read pairs (40.19âGb) with a Q30 of 92.38%. After mapping the Hi-C reads against the assembly of sweet cherry cv. Tieton, 38.91 million valid interaction pairs, accounting for 82.12% of the unique mapped read pairs, were used for the Hi-C analysis. The statistics of the mapping details for the Hi-C data are shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S2</a>. ALLHiC v0.9.12<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Zhang, X., Zhang, S., Zhao, Q., Ming, R. &amp; Tang, H. Assembly of allele-aware, chromosomal-scale autopolyploid genomes based on Hi-C data. Nat. Plants5, 833â845 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1602">14</a></sup> was applied to construct the chromosomal-level scaffolds, and eight chromosomal-level scaffolds were generated, with lengths ranging from 30.63 to 62.32âMb, accounting for 99.59% of the assembly. The statistics for the final pseudochromosomes of the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome are shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#Tab2">2</a>, and the Hi-C contact map is shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#Fig1">1</a>. To the best of our knowledge, this is the first chromosomal-level genome assembly for sweet cherry.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Hi-C interaction heatmap for the sweet cherry (Prunus avium) cv. Tieton genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Hi-C interaction heatmap for the sweet cherry (<i>Prunus avium</i>) cv. Tieton genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00343-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_343_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00343-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_343_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="597" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>The map shows a high resolution of individual chromosomes that are scaffolded and assembled independently</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome assembly quality evaluation</h3><p>The LTR assembly index (LAI), which was first introduced by Ou et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Ou, S., Chen, J. &amp; Jiang, N. Assessing genome assembly quality using the LTR Assembly Index (LAI). Nucleic Acids Res.46, e126 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1643">15</a></sup>, is a newly developed reference-free genome metric that evaluates genome assembly continuity using long-terminal-repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RTs). The LAI is used to compare assembly quality between different species independent of genome size and total scaffold size, and its reliability has been proven in more than 40 plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Ou, S., Chen, J. &amp; Jiang, N. Assessing genome assembly quality using the LTR Assembly Index (LAI). Nucleic Acids Res.46, e126 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1647">15</a></sup>. The LAI and contig N50 results for different genome assemblies within <i>Prunus</i> are shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S3</a>. Among the genome assemblies of sweet cherry cv. Tieton (this study), sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding. DNA Res.24, 499â508 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1657">4</a></sup>, peach v2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Verde, I. et al. The Peach v2.0 release: high-resolution linkage mapping and deep resequencing improve chromosome-scale assembly and contiguity. BMC Genomics.18, 225 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1662">6</a></sup>, flowering cherry (<i>P. yedoensis</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Baek, S. et al. Draft genome sequence of wild Prunus yedoensis reveals massive inter-specific hybridization between sympatric flowering cherries. Genome Biol.19, 127 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1669">9</a></sup>, a flowering cherry interspecific hybrid (âSomei-Yoshinoâ, <i>Cerasus</i>âÃâ<i>yedoensis</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. Phased genome sequence of an interspecific hybrid flowering cherry, âSomei-Yoshinoâ (Cerasus Ã yedoensis). DNA Res.26, 379â389 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1679">10</a></sup>, Chinese plum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zhang, Q. et al. The genome of Prunus mume. Nat. Commun.3, 1318 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1684">5</a></sup>, almond<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Sanchez-Perez, R. et al. Mutation of a bHLH transcription factor allowed almond domestication. Science364, 1095â1098 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1688">8</a></sup>, apricot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jiang, F. et al. The apricot (Prunus armeniaca L.) genome elucidates Rosaceae evolution and beta-carotenoid synthesis. Horticulture Res.6, 128 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1692">11</a></sup>, and European plum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Zhebentyayeva, T. et al. Genetic characterization of worldwide Prunus domestica (plum) germplasm using sequence-based genotyping. Hortic. Res.6, 12 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1696">12</a></sup>, our sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome assembly shows the highest raw LAI (24.45) and LAI (19.68), nearly reaching the âgold standardâ of genome assembly proposed by Ou et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Ou, S., Chen, J. &amp; Jiang, N. Assessing genome assembly quality using the LTR Assembly Index (LAI). Nucleic Acids Res.46, e126 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1700">15</a></sup>. Among other <i>Prunus</i> species, only peach v2.0 (LAIâ=â18.79)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Verde, I. et al. The Peach v2.0 release: high-resolution linkage mapping and deep resequencing improve chromosome-scale assembly and contiguity. BMC Genomics.18, 225 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1708">6</a></sup> and the newly assembled apricot genome (LAIâ=â16.29)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jiang, F. et al. The apricot (Prunus armeniaca L.) genome elucidates Rosaceae evolution and beta-carotenoid synthesis. Horticulture Res.6, 128 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1712">11</a></sup> exhibit similar scores, in agreement with their high assembly qualities. However, the previous genome assembly of sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki presented a raw LAI score of only 2.98 and failed to receive an LAI score due to its fragmented assembly.</p><p>The BUSCO analysis result, as shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#Tab1">1</a>, implies the completeness of our sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome assembly. The NECAT-medaka-NextPolish-Purge_Haplotigs assembly identified 97.4% of the complete BUSCOs, corresponding to the greatest number of BUSCOs among the post-Purge_Haplotigs assemblies. Although the two MaSuRCA assemblies identified more complete BUSCOs, they also identified more duplicated BUSCOs.</p><p>As shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S1</a>, at kmersâ=â37, the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome was estimated to be 340.05âMb in size, with a heterozygosity of 0.409%, which is very close to the genome size of 338âMb estimated by flow cytometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E. D. Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol. Biol. Report.9, 208â218 (1991)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1728">16</a></sup>. The slightly larger genome size of our assembly might be due to the heterozygosity of the sweet cherry genome. In addition, this result indicates the completeness of our cv. Tieton genome assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Annotation of the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome</h3><p>We used homology-based, de novo and RNA-seq methods for protein-coding gene prediction and functional annotation. A total of 38,275 gene models encoding 40,338 proteins were predicted in the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#Tab3">3</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#Fig2">2</a>). A total of 30,416 of 40,338 proteins (75.40%) were annotated by using the EggNOG v4.5.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Huerta-Cepas, J. et al. Fast genome-wide functional annotation through orthology assignment by eggNOG-Mapper. Mol. Biol. Evol.34, 2115â2122 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1746">17</a></sup>, Pfam v32.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="El-Gebali, S. et al. The Pfam protein families database in 2019. Nucleic Acids Res.47, D427âD432 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1750">18</a></sup>, UniProt v2019_10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="UniProt, C. UniProt: a worldwide hub of protein knowledge. Nucleic Acids Res.47, D506âD515 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1754">19</a></sup>, KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kanehisa, M. et al. Data, information, knowledge and principle: back to metabolism in KEGG. Nucleic Acids Res.42, D199âD205 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1759">20</a></sup>, Gene Ontology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Harris, M. A. et al. The Gene Ontology (GO) database and informatics resource. Nucleic Acids Res.32, D258âD261 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1763">21</a></sup>, COG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Koonin, E. V. et al. A comprehensive evolutionary classification of proteins encoded in complete eukaryotic genomes. Genome Biol.5, R7 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1767">22</a></sup>, BUSCO v2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Seppey, M., Manni, M. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness. Methods Mol. Biol.1962, 227â245 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1771">13</a></sup>, MEROPS v12.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Rawlings, N. D. et al. The MEROPS database of proteolytic enzymes, their substrates and inhibitors in 2017 and a comparison with peptidases in the PANTHER database. Nucleic Acids Res.46, D624âD632 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1775">23</a></sup>, Phobius v1.01<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kall, L., Krogh, A. &amp; Sonnhammer, E. L. A combined transmembrane topology and signal peptide prediction method. J. Mol. Biol.338, 1027â1036 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1779">24</a></sup>, SignalP v4.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Almagro Armenteros, J. J. et al. SignalP 5.0 improves signal peptide predictions using deep neural networks. Nat. Biotechnol.37, 420â423 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1784">25</a></sup>, and CAZyme v8.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Lombard, V., Golaconda Ramulu, H., Drula, E., Coutinho, P. M. &amp; Henrissat, B. The carbohydrate-active enzymes database (CAZy) in 2013. Nucleic Acids Res.42, D490âD495 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d37401e1788">26</a></sup> databases or pipelines.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Statistics of gene prediction and functional annotation for the sweet cherry (<i>Prunus avium</i>) cv. Tieton genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Characterization of sweet cherry cv. Tieton and its genome synteny with cv. Satonishiki."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Characterization of sweet cherry cv. Tieton and its genome synteny with cv. Satonishiki.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00343-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_343_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00343-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_343_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="749" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The innermost circle represents ideograms of eight pseudochromosomes of cv. Tieton (on the right side and labeled CHR1âCHR8) and the synteny relationship of gene blocks with the cv. Satonishiki genome (on the left side and labeled chr1âchr8). The middle half-circle represents the heatmap of the repeat content in the cv. Tieton genome. The outermost half-circle represents the heatmap of the gene density in the cv. Tieton genome</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Compared with the former genome annotation of sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding. DNA Res.24, 499â508 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2206">4</a></sup>, our genome annotation of cv. Tieton predicted fewer gene models but with a longer total coding sequence (CDS) length. The CDS size distributions in the two genome annotations are shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S2</a>, and the statistics of the annotation profiles are shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S4</a>. According to Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S2</a>, the genome annotation of sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki predicted more gene models with a length shorter than 650âbp, but our genome annotation of sweet cherry cv. Tieton predicted more gene models with a length longer than 650âbp. There were also more genes annotated by GO and KEGG pathways in our genome annotation of sweet cherry cv. Tieton (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#Tab3">3</a>). These results suggest a better genome assembly and annotation of sweet cherry cv. Tieton than of cv. Satonishiki.</p><p>Repetitive sequence annotation showed a total content of 204.55âMb, indicating that 59.40% of the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome was repetitive (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S5</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#Fig2">2</a>). Among these repetitive elements, LTR retrotransposons (19.71%) were the predominant component (8.50% Copia, followed by 7.59% Gypsy), whereas EnSpm (1.83%) was the most abundant class of DNA transposons. Our sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome exhibits a higher repetitive rate than the genome assembly of cv. Satonishiki (43.8%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding. DNA Res.24, 499â508 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2231">4</a></sup>. The high repetitive rate in the sweet cherry genome may contribute to the poor genome assembly of sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki because short-read technology is limited in resolving highly repetitive sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Paajanen, P. et al. A critical comparison of technologies for a plant genome sequencing project. Gigascience8, giy163 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2235">27</a></sup>.</p><p>The noncoding RNA prediction annotated 3825 noncoding RNAs, including 621 ribosomal RNAs, 1905 transfer RNAs, 1114 small nuclear RNAs, and 131 microRNAs, in the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Synteny analysis between the genome of sweet cherry cv. Tieton and the former draft genome of cv. Satonishiki</h3><p>We performed a synteny analysis between the genomes of sweet cherry cv. Tieton and cv. Satonishiki, and the results are shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#Fig2">2</a>. The genome of sweet cherry cv. Tieton shows good synteny with the genome of cv. Satonishiki. However, because of the fragmented genome assembly of cv. Satonishiki, many genes are not anchored to pseudomolecules, especially in high-repeat-content regions. This result also suggests the good quality of our genome assembly of sweet cherry cv. Tieton.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Evolution of the sweet cherry genome</h3><p>A series of evolutionary analyses were conducted by comparing the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome with the genomes of other representative species in <i>Prunus</i>, including flowering cherry (<i>P. yedoensis</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Baek, S. et al. Draft genome sequence of wild Prunus yedoensis reveals massive inter-specific hybridization between sympatric flowering cherries. Genome Biol.19, 127 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2271">9</a></sup>, peach v2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Verde, I. et al. The Peach v2.0 release: high-resolution linkage mapping and deep resequencing improve chromosome-scale assembly and contiguity. BMC Genomics.18, 225 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2275">6</a></sup>, almond<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Sanchez-Perez, R. et al. Mutation of a bHLH transcription factor allowed almond domestication. Science364, 1095â1098 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2279">8</a></sup>, apricot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jiang, F. et al. The apricot (Prunus armeniaca L.) genome elucidates Rosaceae evolution and beta-carotenoid synthesis. Horticulture Res.6, 128 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2284">11</a></sup>, and Chinese plum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zhang, Q. et al. The genome of Prunus mume. Nat. Commun.3, 1318 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2288">5</a></sup>. Gene family clustering analysis assigned 172,683 proteins to 25,768 orthogroups, accounting for 92.6% (186,437) of the total proteins. The statistics for the orthology analysis results are shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S7</a>. There were 461 species-specific orthogroups in the sweet cherry genome, consisting of 2797 proteins. In addition, 816 orthogroups, accounting for 2850 proteins, were found to be shared between sweet cherry and flowering cherry. GO enrichment analysis of these genes revealed that catalytic and binding processes in the cellular component category and cellular and metabolic processes in the biological process category were the most enriched GO terms. The GO enrichment results are shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S3</a>.</p><p>The gene duplication event analysis revealed that sweet cherry and flowering cherry exhibited the largest numbers of gene duplication events, which were 10,618 and 9659, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S4</a>). However, 1329 gene duplication events were identified in peach, 4611 in apricot, and 3139 in Chinese plum. Although only 18,169 genes were identified in the almond genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Sanchez-Perez, R. et al. Mutation of a bHLH transcription factor allowed almond domestication. Science364, 1095â1098 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2304">8</a></sup>, 2989 gene duplication events were detected.</p><p>We also explored the genome synteny between the sweet cherry cv. Tieton and the other representative species. As shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#Fig3">3</a>, our genome assembly of sweet cherry cv. Tieton exhibits a high level of genome synteny with all the other <i>Prunus</i> genomes, especially peach v2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Verde, I. et al. The Peach v2.0 release: high-resolution linkage mapping and deep resequencing improve chromosome-scale assembly and contiguity. BMC Genomics.18, 225 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2317">6</a></sup>. Only a few blocks were assigned to different chromosomes between our sweet cherry assembly and the peach assembly. Since peach is a genetically well-characterized model for research in <i>Prunus</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Yu, Y. et al. Genome re-sequencing reveals the evolutionary history of peach fruit edibility. Nat. Commun.9, 5404 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2324">28</a></sup> and high-level genome synteny is expected in this genus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Aranzana, M. J. et al. Prunus genetics and applications after de novo genome sequencing: achievements and prospects. Hortic. Res.6, 58 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2329">29</a></sup>, the highest genome synteny with peach v2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Verde, I. et al. The Peach v2.0 release: high-resolution linkage mapping and deep resequencing improve chromosome-scale assembly and contiguity. BMC Genomics.18, 225 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2333">6</a></sup> suggests the good quality of our sweet cherry genome assembly.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Chromosome-level collinearity patterns between sweet cherry cv. Tieton, peach, almond, apricot and Chinese plum."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Chromosome-level collinearity patterns between sweet cherry cv. Tieton, peach, almond, apricot and Chinese plum.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00343-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_343_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00343-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_343_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="370" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The numbers indicate the chromosome order generated from the original genome sequence. Each line represents one block. One block means that more than five paired genes were aligned in sequence. Pav: <i>Prunus avium</i> (sweet cherry), Pp: <i>Prunus persica</i> (peach), Pd: <i>Prunus dulcis</i> (almond), Par: <i>Prunus armeniaca</i> (apricot), Pm: <i>Prunus mume</i> (Chinese plum)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The expansion and contraction of the gene families in sweet cherry cv. Tieton were analyzed by comparing the gene families with those in the other representative species, and the results are shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S4</a>. Compared with those in the other <i>Prunus</i> species, 1489 gene families in the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome were expanded, and 1909 gene families were contracted. The sweet cherry and flowering cherry clade showed more gene family expansions and fewer gene family contractions than the other species in <i>Prunus</i>, with 1157 expanded gene families and 599 contracted gene families.</p><p>Of the 1489 expanded gene families in the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome, 108 were significantly expanded, consisting of 1125 genes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S8</a>). These expanded genes exhibit diverse functions. The 121 genes (10.76%) were annotated in the KEGG (Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes) pathway database and were assigned to 37 pathways (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S9</a>). The five largest pathway categories were alpha-Linolenic acid metabolism (26 genes, ko00592), Ribosome (24 genes, ko03010), Spliceosome (21 genes, ko03040), Plant hormone signal transduction (11 genes, ko04075), and ABC transporters (7 genes, ko02010). In the alpha-linolenic acid metabolism pathways, 26 genes were identified as salicylate carboxymethyltransferase genes in the genome of sweet cherry cv. Tieton. These 26 genes were clustered with another 3 genes in sweet cherry into one gene family, which were identified as salicylic acid carboxyl methyltransferase (SAMT)/jasmonic acid carboxyl methyltransferase (JMT) genes. Only four genes in flowering cherry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Baek, S. et al. Draft genome sequence of wild Prunus yedoensis reveals massive inter-specific hybridization between sympatric flowering cherries. Genome Biol.19, 127 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2394">9</a></sup>, 11 genes in peach v2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Verde, I. et al. The Peach v2.0 release: high-resolution linkage mapping and deep resequencing improve chromosome-scale assembly and contiguity. BMC Genomics.18, 225 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2398">6</a></sup>, three genes in almond<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Sanchez-Perez, R. et al. Mutation of a bHLH transcription factor allowed almond domestication. Science364, 1095â1098 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2402">8</a></sup>, six genes in apricot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jiang, F. et al. The apricot (Prunus armeniaca L.) genome elucidates Rosaceae evolution and beta-carotenoid synthesis. Horticulture Res.6, 128 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2407">11</a></sup>, and seven genes in Chinese plum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zhang, Q. et al. The genome of Prunus mume. Nat. Commun.3, 1318 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2411">5</a></sup> were clustered in this gene family. Its expansion in the sweet cherry genome was mainly caused by two gene duplicate events occurring on chromosome 8. As shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#Fig4">4a</a>, five of eight SAMTs in the peach genome were duplicated two times, corresponding to 20 of 24 SAMTs in the sweet cherry genome. SAMT converts salicylic acid to methyl salicylate, which acts as a long-distance mobile signal for systemic acquired resistance (SAR) in the uninfected part of the plant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Li, Y. X. et al. Salicylic acid in Populus tomentosa is a remote signalling molecule induced by Botryosphaeria dothidea infection. Sci. Rep.8, 14059 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2418">30</a></sup>. The expansion of SAMTs in sweet cherry suggests that salicylic acid may play important roles in the defense response against biotic and abiotic stresses. It is interesting that downstream nonexpressor of pathogenesis-related (NPR) genes in the salicylic acid signaling pathway were also identified in these significantly expanded gene families. In the plant hormone signal transduction pathways, 11 genes were annotated as ankyrin repeat-containing (ANK)-NPR-like proteins, which were clustered with 42 other ANKs in the genome of sweet cherry. ANKs constitute a large multigene family in higher plants and play important roles in proteinâprotein interactions in many developmental processes and abiotic and biotic stress resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Sharma, M. &amp; Pandey, G. K. Expansion and function of repeat domain proteins during stress and development in plants. Front Plant Sci.6, 1218 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2422">31</a></sup>. These 53 ANKs in the sweet cherry genome were clustered with 30 ANKs in flowering cherry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Baek, S. et al. Draft genome sequence of wild Prunus yedoensis reveals massive inter-specific hybridization between sympatric flowering cherries. Genome Biol.19, 127 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2426">9</a></sup>, 42 ANKs in peach v2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Verde, I. et al. The Peach v2.0 release: high-resolution linkage mapping and deep resequencing improve chromosome-scale assembly and contiguity. BMC Genomics.18, 225 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2431">6</a></sup>, 19 ANKs in almond<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Sanchez-Perez, R. et al. Mutation of a bHLH transcription factor allowed almond domestication. Science364, 1095â1098 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2435">8</a></sup>, 20 ANKs in apricot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jiang, F. et al. The apricot (Prunus armeniaca L.) genome elucidates Rosaceae evolution and beta-carotenoid synthesis. Horticulture Res.6, 128 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2439">11</a></sup>, and 50 ANKs in Chinese plum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zhang, Q. et al. The genome of Prunus mume. Nat. Commun.3, 1318 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2443">5</a></sup>. The expansion of ANKs was probably caused by gene duplication on chromosome 2 because 35 ANKs in the sweet cherry genome and 19 ANKs in the peach v2.0 genome were detected on chromosome 2 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#Fig4">4b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Collinearity patterns between the peach v2.0 (Prunus persica) and sweet cherry (Prunus avium) genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Collinearity patterns between the peach v2.0 (<i>Prunus persica</i>) and sweet cherry (<i>Prunus avium</i>) genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00343-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_343_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00343-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_343_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="454" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Collinearity patterns of salicylic acid carboxyl methyltransferase (SAMT) on chromosome 8. The numbers indicate the chromosome position generated from the genome sequence. <b>b</b> Collinearity patterns of ankyrin repeat-containing (ANK) proteins on chromosome 2</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Fruit development-related genes in the sweet cherry genome</h3><p>Using our previously published transcript sequencing data for cv. Tieton fruit development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wei, H. et al. Comparative transcriptome analysis of genes involved in anthocyanin biosynthesis in the red and yellow fruits of sweet cherry (Prunus avium L.). PLoS ONE10, e0121164 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2490">32</a></sup>, we reanalyzed the sequencing data based on the assembled genome. A total of 1044 genes were identified as differentially expressed genes (DEGs, <i>P</i>-values &lt;â0.01) during fruit development. The KEGG pathway enrichment analysis results of these DEGs are shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S10</a>, and the 43 largest categories are shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#Fig5">5</a>. Among these pathways, metabolic pathways (pavi01100), photosynthesis (pavi00195), biosynthesis of secondary metabolites (pavi01110), photosynthesis-antenna proteins (pavi00196), carbon metabolism (pavi01200), carbon fixation in photosynthetic organisms (pavi00710), cysteine and methionine metabolism (pavi00270), plant hormone signal transduction (pavi04075), biosynthesis of amino acids (pavi01230), and Isoquinoline alkaloid biosynthesis (pavi00950) were the 10 largest categories (with the lowest <i>P</i>-values), indicating that these pathways play important roles in the fruit development of sweet cherry cv. Tieton.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="KEGG enrichment analysis of the differentially expressed genes during Tieton fruit development."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: KEGG enrichment analysis of the differentially expressed genes during Tieton fruit development.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00343-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_343_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00343-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_343_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="636" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>The size of the dot represents the gene count. The color represents the <i>P</i>-value</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Among all the DEGs, 26 genes were involved in plant hormone signal transduction pathways. Twelve of them were involved in the auxin signaling pathway, and 9 of them were identified as abscisic acid (ABA)-responsive genes. In comparison with the well-known function of ABA-responsive genes in the fruit development of sweet cherry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Shen, X. J. et al. A role for PacMYBA in ABA-regulated anthocyanin biosynthesis in red-colored sweet cherry cv. Hong Deng (Prunus avium L.). Plant Cell Physiol.55, 862â880 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2533">33</a></sup>, how auxin-responsive genes are involved in this process is still unclear. Of these auxin-responsive genes, six were identified as <i>AUX/IAA</i> (<i>auxin-responsive protein IAA</i>), two were identified as <i>GH3</i> (<i>indole-3-acetic acid-amido synthetase GH3</i>), two were identified as <i>SAUR</i> (<i>small auxin-up RNA</i>), and two were identified as <i>ARF</i> (auxin response factor). The expression patterns of these genes are shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#Fig6">6</a>. It is known that the application of exogenous auxin can delay fruit development, and the endogenous auxin level decreases during the fruit ripening process in sweet cherry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Teribia, N., Tijero, V. &amp; Munne-Bosch, S. Linking hormonal profiles with variations in sugar and anthocyanin contents during the natural development and ripening of sweet cherries. N. Biotechnol.33, 824â833 (2016)." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2562">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, Y. P. et al. Transcriptional regulation of PaPYLs, PaPP2Cs and PaSnRK2s during sweet cherry fruit development and in response to abscisic acid and auxin at onset of fruit ripening. Plant Growth Regul.75, 455â464 (2015)." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2562_1">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Stern, R. A., Flaishman, M., Applebaum, S. &amp; Ben-Arie, R. Effect of synthetic auxins on fruit development of âBingâ cherry (Prunus avium L.). Sci. Horticulturae.114, 275â280 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2565">36</a></sup>. The gene expression patterns of the <i>AUX/IAA</i>s and <i>ARF</i>s were consistent with the endogenous auxin level during fruit development. However, the increased expression of <i>GH3</i>s and <i>SAUR</i>s in the late stage of fruit development reveals a complicated interaction between auxin and the sweet cherry ripening process. Further studies are needed to explore the function of auxin in fruit development in sweet cherry. Three members of <i>TCH4</i> (xyloglucan endotransglucosylase/hydrolase protein) involved in the brassinosteroid signal transduction pathway were identified as DEGs, which were mainly expressed in the middle stage of fruit development in sweet cherry. One jasmonate ZIM domain-containing protein (identified as <i>TIFY 9</i>) was increased during the fruit ripening process, which confirms that jasmonic acid is involved in this process in sweet cherry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Berni, R., Cai, G., Xu, X., Hausman, J. F. &amp; Guerriero, G. Identification of jasmonic acid biosynthetic genes in sweet cherry and expression analysis in four ancient varieties from tuscany. Int. J. Mol. Sci.20, 10 (2019)." href="#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2588">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kondo, S., Motoyama, M., Michiyama, H. &amp; Kim, M. Roles of jasmonic acid in the development of sweet cherries as measured from fruit or disc samples. Plant Growth Regul.37, 37â44 (2002)." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2588_1">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Kondo, S., Tomiyama, A. &amp; Seto, H. Changes of endogenous jasmonic acid and methyl jasmonate in apples and sweet cherries during fruit development. J. Am. Soc. Horticultural Sci.125, 282â287 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2591">39</a></sup>. One <i>AHP</i> (histidine-containing phosphotransfer protein 1-like) was decreased during this process, consistent with the endogenous cytokinin level changes in sweet cherry fruit development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Teribia, N., Tijero, V. &amp; Munne-Bosch, S. Linking hormonal profiles with variations in sugar and anthocyanin contents during the natural development and ripening of sweet cherries. N. Biotechnol.33, 824â833 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2599">34</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Expression patterns of plant hormone signal transduction pathway genes involved in sweet cherry fruit development."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Expression patterns of plant hormone signal transduction pathway genes involved in sweet cherry fruit development.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00343-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_343_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00343-8/MediaObjects/41438_2020_343_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="423" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>25D: 25 days after flowering, 35D: 35 days after flowering, 45D: 45 days after flowering, 55D: 55 days after flowering</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Resistance-related genes in the sweet cherry genome</h3><p>The sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome consisted of 772 resistance-related (<i>R</i>) genes with leucine-rich repeat (LRR) domains and/or nucleotide-binding sites (NBSs). These genes were classified into five groups: <i>coiled-coil-NBS-LRR</i> (<i>CNL</i>), <i>TIR-NBS-LRR</i> (<i>TNL</i>), NBS-LRR (<i>NL</i>), <i>LRR</i>, and <i>NBS</i>. <i>LRR</i> was the largest group, with 506 genes, whereas <i>CNL</i> included 148 genes, <i>TNL</i> included 31 genes, NL included 229 genes, and NBS included 44 genes. Compared to the peach genome, with 437 <i>R</i> genes, the sweet cherry genome had more <i>R</i> genes (772). The sweet cherry genome had more LRR domain-containing proteins, whereas the peach genome had more TNL-encoding genes (146 genes vs. 31 genes).</p><p>We also conducted chromosome-level genome synteny analysis of the <i>R</i> genes in the sweet cherry and peach genomes. As shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S5</a>, the majority of the <i>R</i> genes in the sweet cherry genome showed good synteny with those in the peach genome. In addition, these <i>R</i> genes were unevenly distributed along the eight chromosomes of sweet cherry. Their hotspots resided on chromosomes 1, 2, and 8, which are clearly shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#MOESM1">S6</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Using the combination of Oxford Nanopore technology, Illumina short reads and Hi-C scaffolding, we successfully assembled a high-quality reference genome for sweet cherry cv. Tieton and anchored 99.59% of the sequences to eight pseudochromosomes. According to the LAI-based method for assessing genome assembly quality, our sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome assembly shows better assembly quality than all the documented genomes within <i>Prunus</i>. This genome assembly can serve as a high-quality reference genome for sweet cherry to support studies of molecular breeding, genetics and evolution in sweet cherry and the <i>Prunus</i> genus.</p><p>Compared with the previous draft assembly of sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki, our genome assembly of cv. Tieton provides higher coverage and better continuity. First, the assembly size for sweet cherry cv. Tieton has been increased to 344.29âMb, representing 101.2% of the estimated genome size. Second, our assembly of cv. Tieton exhibits a 15.47x longer contig N50 than cv. Satonishiki. We also annotated more genes than were included in the former annotation of cv. Satonishiki, suggesting the better continuity of our assembly.</p><p>The repetitive rate in the genome of sweet cherry cv. Tieton (59.40%) is higher than that in all the other sequenced species of <i>Prunus</i>, such as almond (34.6%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Sanchez-Perez, R. et al. Mutation of a bHLH transcription factor allowed almond domestication. Science364, 1095â1098 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2713">8</a></sup>, peach (37.14%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="International Peach Genome, I. et al. The high-quality draft genome of peach (Prunus persica) identifies unique patterns of genetic diversity, domestication and genome evolution. Nat. Genet.45, 487â494 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2717">7</a></sup>, apricot (38.28%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jiang, F. et al. The apricot (Prunus armeniaca L.) genome elucidates Rosaceae evolution and beta-carotenoid synthesis. Horticulture Res.6, 128 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2721">11</a></sup>, Chinese plum (45.0%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zhang, Q. et al. The genome of Prunus mume. Nat. Commun.3, 1318 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00343-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d37401e2725">5</a></sup>, and flowering cherry (<i>P. yedoensis</i>, 47.2%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Baek, S. et al. Draft genome sequence of wild Prunus yedoensis reveals massive inter-specific hybridization between sympatric flowering cherries.
